The great Bobby Fischer put out a "How to play Chess" book back in the 1980s.  Along with the various instructions on the mechanics of the game, there were examples of moves that he made against opponents during his many tournaments, and one of them was exactly this. His opponent opened up his King just like Schlatt did, and Bobby won the round in two moves.  Ever since reading that book, I have waited nearly 40 years to have an opponent open up their King like that, and no one, to my great disappointment, ever did.  To see this, now, was amazing!

The fool's mate received its name because it can occur only if White commits an extraordinary blunder.   1. f3 e6 2. g4?? Qh4#  The fool's mate can be achieved in two moves only by Black, giving checkmate on the second move with the queen. Black can be mated in an analogous way, although this requires an additional move, with White's queen delivering checkmate on the third move. Even among rank beginners, this checkmate rarely occurs in practice.  The mate is an illustration of the kingside weakness shared by both players along the f- and g-files during the opening phase of the game. A player may also suffer an early checkmate if the f- and g-pawns are advanced prematurely and the kingside is not properly defended, as shown in historical miniature games recorded in chess literature.

Of the twenty possible first moves in chess, author and grandmaster Edmar Mednis argues that 1.f3 is the worst. Grandmaster Benjamin Finegold teaches "Never play f3". In his text on openings, Paul van der Sterren considered 1. f3 beneath mention by name:  "Then there are those moves with which White really tries to shoot himself in the foot, like 1. f3 or 1. g4. Schlatt's Gambit 1. g4, e5 2. f4  An outstanding chess opening that allows the white to sacrifice their king for a bishop development (Modified Grob's opening).  There's 2 variations of the opening: Schlatt's Gambit: Accepted 1. g4, e5 2. f4, Qh4# (Botez vs Schaltt game, 1-0). Schlatt's Gambit: Declined Second black move is not Qh4#

Right people this is indeed called the FOOL'S MATE of just 2 moves...The second shortest way to checkmate someone is to checkmate them in only 3 moves... The third way to checkmate someone quickly, is called the Scholar's Mate and it's only 4 moves... During my high school years, a number of years ago, I was a self taught chess-master who had been lucky enough to meet and play some rated chess-masters whom I defeated much to their shock and surprise. Including one at my local YMCA who was the very first one I met and played. Now, I was only just going in to quietly watch games being played; with the sole intention of increasing my knowledge of the game. But, just moments after entering the YMCA Chess Club, a highly rated Chess Master ask me if I'd like to play a game with him. I accepted, telling him it would be an honour to do so, and a great opportunity to learn more about the game from him. Such was not the case, because I defeated that Chess Master to his utter and complete shock. To add insult to injury, the Director of the chess club immediately came over to our table, and told that highly rated Chess Master that if this had been a tournament Game.. he would have LOST HIS RATING to me! Neither the Director nor I were prepared for the reaction of that Chessmaster upon hearing those words... That Chessmaster quickly rose to his feet, and in a violent rage, knocked all the chess pieces and the chessboard right off the table and onto the floor... then he angrily stormed out of the Chess Club...By the way, if anyone wants to know, there's also a 7 move checkmate...To the guy who played and lost this game in such a pathetic manner; I suggest you read the following two chess books; (1.) An Invitation To Chess... and ...(2.) Masters Of The Board...
